Refuting the Objection of Compulsion and Divine Weakness
Nasafi refutes the objection of compulsion and divine weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the objection that the Maturidi position on human action leads to compulsion or divine inability.

Core Idea
The Maturidi view preserves both divine power and human choice.

Nasafi now takes up a common objection against the Maturidi position on human action. Critics argue that if God creates all actions, humans are either compelled or God is limited. In this passage, Nasafi shows why the objection fails, grounding his response in the established proofs of God's creative power and human acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first paragraph of the passage, which presents the opponents' argument. Note the two horns of the dilemma: either the servant has power to refuse (making God incapable) or the servant is compelled.
The objection is based on the assumption that if God creates the act, the servant cannot have real power.
2
Identify Nasafi's initial reply
Nasafi responds by stating that they have already established by proof that God is the creator and the servant is an acquirer. The objection would require invalidating one of these certain proofs, which is impossible.
Nasafi does not re-prove the position; he asserts its certainty.
3
Understand the distinction between movements
Nasafi distinguishes necessary movement (where the servant has no power of refusal) from voluntary movement (where the servant wills and chooses). For voluntary actions, refusal is impossible because the act exists concurrent with the servant's power.
This distinction shows that compulsion applies only to necessary actions, not to voluntary ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا الوجه يبطل أيضًا قولهم إن ما قلتم يؤدي إلى جعل العباد مضطرين أو إلى تعجيز الله تعالى فإن الله تعالى إذا أراد أن يخلق كسب العبد إن كان للعبد قوة الامتناع فقد عجز الله تعالى عن تخليقه وإن لم يكن له قدرة الامتناع فهو إذًا مضطر

فإنا نقول: ثبتنا بالدليل أن لا اختراع من قبل العبد وأن له فعلاً كان مجموع الدليلين موجبًا كونه تعالى قادرًا مخترعًا، وكون العبد فاعلاً مكتسبًا مختارا.

وما قلتم من السؤال يوجب بطلان أحد هذين الوجهين، وما ثبت بالدليل المتيقن غير محتمل للبطلان، فإذا كان السؤال في نفسه باطلاً.

ثم نقول: إن كان الله تعالى أراد الحركة الضرورية من العبد فيخلقه ولا يكون للعبد قدرة الامتناع والله تعالى قادر والعبد مضطر، وإن أراد تخليق الحركة الاختيارية فالعبد لا يتصور منه الامتناع لأنه تعالى إنما يخلق تلك الحركة إذا أرادها العبد واختار وقصد اكتسابها فيخلق الله تعالى لا محالة لإجرائه العادة في تخليقه عند وجود قصد العبد لا لكونه ملجأ إلى ذلك، على أن الله تعالى إذا كان يخلقه مقارنا لقدرة العبد كيف يتصور منه الامتناع عن الفعل وهو موجود والامتناع عن الموجود محال، والله الموفق.
And in this way their statement is also invalidated: What you say leads to making servants compelled or to making God incapable. For if God intends to create the acquisition (kasb) of the servant, if the servant has the power of refusal, then God is incapable of creating it, and if he does not have the power of refusal, then he is compelled.

So we say: We have established by proof that there is no creation from the servant, and that he has an act that is a combination of two proofs: that He (God) is powerful and creator, and that the servant is an acting, acquiring, choosing agent.

And what you said in the objection necessitates the invalidation of one of these two aspects. And what is established by certain proof is not subject to invalidation. So the objection is itself invalid.

Then we say: If God intends the necessary movement from the servant, He creates it, and the servant does not have the power of refusal, and God is powerful and the servant is compelled. And if He intends to create the voluntary movement, then the servant cannot be imagined to refuse, because He only creates that movement when the servant wills it and chooses and intends to acquire it. So God creates it necessarily due to His customary practice of creating it upon the existence of the servant's intention, not because He is compelled to do so. Moreover, since God creates it concurrent with the servant's power, how can refusal be imagined from him while the act exists? And refusal of something existent is impossible. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection from opponents (likely Mu'tazila) who argue that the Maturidi view (that human actions are created by God and acquired by humans) leads to either compulsion of humans or making God incapable. The author first presents the objection: if God wants to create the servant's kasb (acquisition), if the servant has the power to refuse (quwwat al-imtina'), then God is incapable of creating it; if not, then the servant is compelled. The author responds by asserting that they have already proven by proof (dalil) that there is no creation (ikhtira') from the servant, yet the servant has an act that is a combination of two proofs (majmu' al-dalilayn): God is the powerful creator, and the servant is an acting, acquiring, choosing agent. The objection would require invalidating one of these two established aspects, but they are proven by certain proof (al-dalil al-mutayaqqan), so the objection is invalid. He then elaborates: If God intends necessary motion (al-haraka al-daruriyya), the servant has no power of refusal, so the servant is compelled (but that is not the case for voluntary actions). For voluntary actions (al-haraka al-ikhtiyariyya), God creates the movement only when the servant wills it and intends to acquire it, so refusal is impossible because the act exists concurrent with the servant's power, and refusal of an existent is impossible. Thus, the objection is based on a misunderstanding of the Maturidi position.

Key Terms
مضطر
تعجيز
كسب
اختراع
قوة الامتناع
حركة ضرورية
حركة اختيارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidism negates human freedom and makes humans compelled.
Correction: Nasafi distinguishes necessary movements (where compulsion is acknowledged) from voluntary movements, where the servant genuinely wills and chooses, and God creates the act concurrent with that choice.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does the objection about compulsion fail?
A) Because the servant has independent creative power
B) Because the established proof of servant's acquisition and God's creation is certain and cannot be invalidated
C) Because God is not capable of creating human actions
D) Because the objection is not logical

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the objection is based on a misunderstanding: voluntary actions involve genuine choice and acquisition, not compulsion, and the certainty of the established proofs makes the objection invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'God made me do it' or 'It was out of my control'? How does this lesson’s distinction between necessary and voluntary movements challenge that excuse? Will you now own your choices fully, even when they are wrong?
?
If you truly believe that every good deed you do is a creation of Allah that He brings into existence concurrent with your intention, does that fill you with humility or pride? How can you cultivate gratitude for the ability to acquire good deeds, knowing that the act itself is from Allah?
?
This lesson says that for voluntary actions, refusal is impossible because the act exists with your power. Does your daily experience of hesitation or regret before sinning contradict this? Are you willing to admit that your 'feeling of being forced' is often a cover for your own choice? What will you do the next time you feel tempted to blame your circumstances for a sin?

One Act Under Two Powers: Responding to the Mu'tazila
Al-Nasafi proves one act under two powers and questions the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that a single act can be under both God's creative power and the servant's acquired power, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
A single act can be created by God and acquired by the servant.

The Mu'tazila argued that one act cannot be under two powers. Imam al-Nasafi responds by clarifying that the act is under God's creative power and the servant's acquired power — two different aspects. This lesson walks through his proof and the key refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow, then read the literal translation. Note how the author builds his argument.
The passage is dense but follows a clear logical order.
2
Identify the two aspects of power
Look for the distinction between 'invention' (ikhtira') and 'acquisition' (iktisab). This distinction is key to the argument.
The author agrees impossibility if both powers are of the same kind.
3
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
See how the author questions the Mu'tazila: they have no proof except imagination (wahm). They must specify which aspect they mean.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالوقوف على ما بينا من دليل استحالة ثبوت قدرة التخليق للعبد، ودليل ثبوت الفعل والقدرة له يعرف جواز دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين بحمد الله تعالى دليل فيه كفاية وغنية عن الاشتغال بغيره من الدلائل. ثم نقول: قد سبق منا القول في الفصل الذي أثبتنا فيه استحالة دخول الاختراع تحت قدرة العبد، وأن إقدار الغير على ما لا يقدر عليه المقدر محال، وقد انعقد الإجماع أن الله تعالى هو الذي يقدر العبد ويخلق قدرته، وأن القدرة ليست بداخله تحت قدرة العبد إذ لو منعت منه لما تمكن من اكتسابها عندنا وتخليقها عندهم، وإذا كان الله تعالى هو الذي يقدر العبد كان محالاً أن يقدره على ما لا قدرة له عليه على ما قررنا، فكان ذلك دليلاً على جواز دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين، ثم لا دليل يدل للمعتزلة على استحالة دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين، إلا أنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك وهم أبدا يفزعون إلى الوهم ويجعلونه عيارا للعقل والفهم وهو بعيد عن الصواب على ما قررنا. ثم قيل لهم أيش تعنون بقولكم إن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قاردين محال ؟ أتعنون دخوله من جهة الاختراع أو من جهة الاكتساب أو من جهة اكتساب و اختراع؟ فإن قالوا من جهة الاختراع فسلم تنازعهم في هذا، وإن قالوا: من جهة الاكتساب فكذلك لأن فعلاً واحدًا لا يكون فعلا لكاسبين. وإن قالوا من جهة الاكتساب والاختراع أن يقدر على اختراعه مخترع وعلى اكتسابه مكتسب ففيه النزاع.
And by considering what we have explained of the proof of the impossibility of establishing the power of invention (takhliq) for the servant, and the proof of the establishment of action and power for him, one knows the permissibility of one act of power (maqdur) falling under two powers (qadirayn), by the praise of God, a proof sufficient and independent of engaging with other proofs. Then we say: We have already stated in the chapter in which we established the impossibility of invention (ikhtira') falling under the servant's power, and that empowering another over what the empowerer cannot do is impossible. And consensus has been reached that God, exalted, is the one who empowers the servant and creates his power, and that power (qudra) is itself not within the servant's power—for if it were prevented from him, he would not be able to acquire it according to us, nor create it according to them. And if God is the one who empowers the servant, it is impossible that He empower him over what he has no power over, as we have established. So that was a proof for the permissibility of one act of power falling under two powers. Then there is no proof for the Mu'tazila indicating the impossibility of one act of power falling under two powers, except that they have not witnessed that, and they always flee to imagination (wahm) and make it the criterion for intellect and understanding, and that is far from correctness as we have established. Then it was said to them: What do you mean by your statement that the entry of one act of power under two powers is impossible? Do you mean its entry from the aspect of invention (ikhtira'), or from the aspect of acquisition (iktisab), or from the aspect of acquisition and invention? If they say from the aspect of invention, then we concede their dispute in this. And if they say from the aspect of acquisition, likewise because a single act cannot be an act for two acquirers. And if they say from the aspect of acquisition and invention, i.e., that an inventor has power over its invention and an acquirer over its acquisition, then therein lies the dispute.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a proof and a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that one act cannot be under two powers. The author begins by stating that considering the earlier proofs—that the servant cannot have creative power (takhliq) but does have action and power—leads to the conclusion that one act of power (maqdur) can be under two powers. This proof is sufficient and needs no other. Then he reiterates that invention (ikhtira') is impossible under the servant's power, and empowering someone over what the empowerer cannot do is impossible. Consensus holds that God empowers the servant and creates his power; the servant's power itself is not under his control. Since God empowers the servant, He cannot empower him over what he has no power over (i.e., creation). Thus one act can be under God's creative power and the servant's acquired power. The Mu'tazila have no proof against this; they rely on imagination (wahm) as a criterion, which is erroneous. Then the author poses a question: What exactly do you mean by impossibility? If you mean from the aspect of invention, we agree; if from acquisition, also agreed (one act cannot be acquired by two). But if you mean from both aspects—invented by one and acquired by another—that is the point of dispute, and our proofs support it.

Key Terms
مقدور
قدرة
قادران
اختراع
اكتساب
وهم
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is always impossible for one act to be under two powers.
Correction: It is only impossible if both powers are of the same kind (both creative or both acquisitive). It is possible if one is creative and the other acquisitive.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the Mu'tazila's faulty criterion for rejecting one act under two powers?
A) Consensus
B) Imagination (wahm)
C) Reason
D) Scripture

Key Takeaway
One act can be under two powers as long as the powers are of different types: creative (God) and acquisitive (servant).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something good, do you secretly believe it was entirely your own doing, or do you truly feel that God enabled you? How might this lesson's distinction between creation and acquisition change your next prayer of gratitude?
?
The Mu'tazila relied on what seemed plausible to their imagination rather than on divine revelation and sound reasoning. In what area of your faith are you tempted to trust your own assumptions over what God has clearly taught? Will you actively submit that area to the truth of this lesson today?
?
If God creates both the act and your power to acquire it, then your will is not independent—yet you are still responsible. Does this truth humble you or trouble you? How will you live differently now that you know your every good deed is a gift both created by God and acquired by you?

The Trilemma on Created Acts
Nasafi presents a trilemma for the opponent on generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical trilemma that the Maturidi author uses to force the opponent to either accept the Sunni position on created acts or face logical inconsistency.

Core Idea
The opponent must choose: accept Sunni doctrine, accept dual agency, or be stubborn—none consistent with their own premises.

Imam Nasafi presents a decisive logical trilemma to the opponent who denies that God creates all human acts. The trilemma shows that the opponent's own principles lead to an unavoidable choice: either recant, contradict themselves, or persist in pure obstinacy. This argument powerfully defends the Sunni position on divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the trilemma passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author addresses the opponent directly using 'you'.
The passage is a single sentence but packs a complete argument.
2
Identify the three options
Find the three options the author presents: (1) recant and adopt the Sunni view, (2) accept dual agency (two powers on one act), (3) cling to both doctrines stubbornly.
Each option is introduced with 'إما أن'.
3
Understand the logical force
For each option, consider why it is problematic for the opponent. The first requires abandoning their doctrine, the second contradicts their own principle that only one agent acts, the third is irrational obstinacy.
4
Reflect on the defense of Sunni doctrine
Realize that the trilemma demonstrates that the Sunni position (all acts from God) is the only logically consistent stance within the opponent's own framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك إنك بين أمور ثلاثة: إما أن ترجع عن مسألة المتولدات وتجعلها الله تعالى كما هو مذهب أهل السنة، أو تجيز تعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد وجعل شيء فعلاً لفاعلين وفيه رجوع عن مذهبك، أو تتمسك بالمذهبين مع لزوم الدليل فتوصف بالعناد المحض، والله الموفق.
Then after that you are among three matters: either you recant on the issue of generated acts and attribute them to God Almighty as is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna, or you permit the attachment of two powers to a single possible object and make something an act of two agents – and in that is a retraction from your doctrine, or you adhere to both doctrines while the proof necessitates [contradiction] and you are described as pure obstinacy. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a logical trilemma presented to the opponent (likely a Mu'tazilite) who holds that acts are generated by creatures and that God's power does not directly produce human acts. The author gives three options: (1) Abandon the doctrine of generated acts (mutawallidat) and attribute all acts to God, as Ahl al-Sunna do. (2) Accept that two powers can attach to one act (i.e., both God and the creature act) – but this contradicts the opponent's own principle. (3) Cling stubbornly to both positions despite the logical consequence, which is pure obstinacy. The tone is conclusive, forcing the opponent to concede.

Key Terms

قدرتين
مقدور واحد
فاعلين
عناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The trilemma is a rhetorical trick with no real logical force.
Correction: The trilemma is a valid logical deduction from the opponent's own premises; each option follows necessarily from the opponent's commitments.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three options the author presents to the opponent?
A) Return to the Sunni position on created acts.
B) Deny the existence of God altogether.
C) Accept that two powers can act on one object.
D) Insist stubbornly on both doctrines.

Key Takeaway
The trilemma shows that the Mu'tazilite position on created acts is logically untenable; the only consistent position is the Sunni doctrine that God creates all acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

How does your pride or desire for consistency prevent you from saying, 'I was wrong; I now accept God's word completely'? Ask Allah for the strength to be among those who recant falsehood without hesitation.
?
This passage ends with 'والله الموفق'—success is from God alone. Have you ever sought guidance sincerely when faced with a difficult theological or moral choice, or did you rely on your own reasoning? 

Commit today to make duas for divine guidance in matters where your intellect alone cannot reach certainty, especially in issues of 'aqeedah.

Al-Murdar on Joint Action and Moral Attributes
Al-Murdar's doctrine on joint agency and moral attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Murdar's doctrine that a single act can be attributed to two agents with different moral evaluations, and how this relates to the problem of divine creation of human acts.

Core Idea
One act, two agents, two opposite moral judgments.

Al-Murdar, a Mu'tazilite theologian, faced the challenge of reconciling divine omnipotence with human moral responsibility. In this passage, he proposes a radical solution: a single movement can be the act of two agents, and its moral value depends on the agent's command or prohibition. This view attempts to preserve God's wisdom while holding humans accountable for sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source text carefully. Notice the structure: first the general claim, then two scenarios illustrating the claim.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Examine the first scenario: two humans
Identify how al-Murdar says one movement can be both obedience and disobedience depending on the agent's command or prohibition.
This shows the moral asymmetry within a single act.
3
Examine the second scenario: human and wind
Here the second agent is the wind, which is an act of God. Al-Murdar attributes the movement to both God and the human, but the human is sinful while God is not.
This distinguishes between moral and creative agency.
4
Understand the theological implication
Reflect on how al-Murdar's view resolves the problem of God creating sinful acts: God does that which is disobedience from the human, but He is not disobedient.
This removes the objection against Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو موسى بن صبح المردار المعروف بناسك البغداديين أن الحركة فعلهما جميعًا ومقدورهما ولو كان أحد المحركين مأمورا به والآخر منهيًا عنه كانت الحركة طاعة من المأمور به حسنا، معصية من المنهي عنه قبيحًا، ولو كان أحد المحركين إنسانًا منهيًا عن التحريك والآخر الريح كانت الحركة فعل الله تعالى وفعل العبد، وهي معصية قبيحة من العبد، وهي حكمة من الصانع القديم جل وعلا، وكان القديم فعل ما هو معصية من غيره ولم يكن بذلك عاصيًا، وكذا لو كان الرجل مأمورًا بذلك كان الله تعالى فعل ما هو طاعة من العبد، ولم يكن بذلك مطيعا وعلى هذا لم يبق له في مسألة خلق الأفعال مع أهل الحق كلام.
And Abu Musa ibn Subh al-Murdar, known as the ascetic of the Baghdadis, claimed that the movement is the act of both and their potential object. If one of the movers is commanded [to move] and the other forbidden, then the movement is obedience from the one commanded, good, and disobedience from the one forbidden, evil. And if one of the movers is a human forbidden from moving and the other is the wind, then the movement is the act of God Almighty and the act of the servant; it is evil disobedience from the servant, and it is wisdom from the ancient Maker, Exalted and High. And the Ancient did that which is disobedience from another, but He is not thereby disobedient. Likewise, if the man was commanded to do that, then God Almighty did that which is obedience from the servant, and He is not thereby obedient. And on this basis, he no longer has any argument with the people of truth on the issue of the creation of acts.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage reports the doctrine of al-Murdar, a Mu'tazilite theologian. He asserts that a single movement can be the act of two agents (e.g., two humans, or a human and the wind) and that it can have different moral attributes depending on the agent's command: it is good/obedience for the one commanded, evil/disobedience for the one forbidden. In the case of human and wind, the movement is attributed to both God (as creator of the wind) and the human, but God is not sinful despite the act being sin from the human's perspective. Similarly, if the human is commanded, God does that which is obedience for the human but is not obedient Himself. Al-Murdar argues that this resolves the problem of divine creation of human acts, leaving no further dispute with Ahl al-Sunna.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Murdar's view implies that God directly commits sin when He creates a sinful act.
Correction: Al-Murdar distinguishes between the act as moral for the human (sin) and as creative act for God (wisdom). God is not morally evaluated by the same standard as humans.

Checkpoint
According to al-Murdar, if a human is forbidden to move and the wind moves the object, the movement is:
A) Only God's act and not the human's.
B) Both God's act (through wind) and the human's act, with the human sinning.
C) Only the human's act, and God has no role.
D) Neither God's nor the human's act, but a natural event.

Key Takeaway
Al-Murdar argues that a single act can be attributed to two agents and can be simultaneously good and evil depending on the agent's command, preserving God's wisdom while holding humans accountable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Al-Murdar argues that the same movement can be obedience for one and disobedience for another based on command. In your life, are there actions that you justify because 'everyone does it' or because the outcome was good? How does this passage force you to examine not just the action, but whether you are commanded or forbidden? Commit to one area where you will obey God's command even if the world sees it differently.
?

What specific situation in your life right now requires you to trust that God's wisdom is behind what seems like a wrong turn? Will you surrender that to Him tonight?

A Reductio Argument Against Two Agents on One Act
Al-Nasafi presents a reductio argument against the Mu'tazilite position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi uses a reductio argument to force the Mu'tazilite position on generated acts into three unacceptable options.

Core Idea
The opponent's own logic is turned against him, leaving no escape.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a masterful reductio argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that a single act cannot be caused by two agents. He shows that their reasoning leads to an absurdity, and then presents them with three choices, each contradicting their own theology. This lesson walks through the argument step by step, revealing the strength of the Ahl al-Sunna position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical structure: premise, analogy, then three options.
2
Analyze the reductio analogy
Identify how Al-Nasafi draws an analogy between two equal agents moving a stone and God creating two equal persons. Understand why the opponent is forced to concede the same impossibility.
The key is that the opponent's own premise is applied universally.
3
Examine the three forced options
Read each option carefully and consider what it means for the opponent's doctrine. Think about why each option is problematic for a Mu'tazilite.
The first option aligns with Ahl al-Sunna, the second contradicts Mu'tazilite teachings, and the third leads to pure stubbornness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: إذا قلت يستحيل أن يتفق الاثنان على التحريك عند استوائهما في المعاني لأنه يؤدي إلى محال فقل يستحيل أن يخلق الله شخصين مستويي القدرة والبنية لأنه يؤدي إلى محال إذ لا فرق بينهما. ثم بعد ذلك إنك بين أمور ثلاثة: إما أن ترجع عن مسألة المتولدات وتجعلها الله تعالى كما هو مذهب أهل السنة، أو تجيز تعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد وجعل شيء فعلاً لفاعلين وفيه رجوع عن مذهبك، أو تتمسك بالمذهبين مع لزوم الدليل فتوصف بالعناد المحض، والله الموفق.
Then it is said to him: If you say that it is impossible for two [agents] to agree upon moving [a thing] when they are equal in attributes because it leads to an impossibility, then say that it is impossible for God to create two persons equal in power and constitution because it leads to an impossibility, since there is no difference between them. Then after that, you are among three matters: either you retract from the issue of generated acts (mutawallidāt) and attribute them to God Almighty as is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna, or you allow the connection of two powers (qudra) to a single object of power (maqdūr) and make a thing an act of two agents—and in that is a retraction from your doctrine—or you adhere to both doctrines while the proof is binding, and thus you are described with pure stubbornness. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against a Mu'tazilite opponent who denies that a single act can be produced by two agents. The argument draws an analogy: if the opponent claims it is impossible for two men to together move a stone when they are equal in attributes because it leads to an impossibility, then he must also say it is impossible for God to create two persons equal in power and constitution for the same reason, since there is no difference between the two cases. Then the opponent is given three options, each problematic for his position. The first option is to abandon the doctrine of generated acts (mutawallidāt) and attribute them solely to God, which is the orthodox Ash'ari/Maturidi position. The second option is to accept that two powers can be connected to a single object of power, which contradicts his own Mu'tazilite school. The third option is to stubbornly hold both contradictory positions, which would characterize him as pure obstinacy. The argument ultimately forces the opponent into acknowledging the orthodox position or exposing his inconsistency.

Key Terms
تحريك
استواء
محال
متولدات
قدرة
مقدور
عناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that two equal agents cannot act together in reality.
Correction: The argument only uses the opponent's own premise to force a contradiction; it does not assert that as a fact.

Checkpoint
What are the three options presented to the opponent in this passage?
A) Retract from mutawallidat, allow two powers on one act, or stubbornly hold both doctrines.
B) Accept qadar, reject free will, or become an atheist.
C) Deny God's power, affirm human freedom, or accept determinism.
D) Return to Ahl al-Sunna, become a Mu'tazilite, or remain silent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's reductio argument shows that the Mu'tazilite position on generated acts is logically untenable, forcing a choice between orthodoxy, contradiction, or obstinacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When faced with a logical proof that contradicts a belief you hold dear, do you tend to retract, adjust, or persist stubbornly? Which of the three options from this passage most often describes your response? What would change if you truly submitted to the truth, even when it humbles you?

Al-Nasafi ends with 'And God is the Grantor of success.' What does this teach you about the role of divine guidance in accepting truth? Have ever you recognized that your own stubbornness was preventing you from seeing the truth? What would it take for you to sincerely ask God to grant you success in abandoning a false position?
?
The opponent is forced into a dilemma where every option requires abandoning his doctrine. Have you ever been in a situation where your intellectual commitments left you with no honorable escape? Did you choose humility or pride? What would it look like for you to embrace the path of Ahl al-Sunna—complete reliance on God's power—in your daily life?

Al-Mirdar's Claim: One Act, Two Moralities
Al-Nasafi reports and refutes al-Mirdar's view that a single act can be both obedience and disobedience from different agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Mirdar's controversial view that a single act can be simultaneously obedience and disobedience from different agents, and why al-Nasafi rejects it.

Core Idea
A single act cannot be simultaneously obedience and disobedience—it depends on the agent.

The Mu'tazilite theologian al-Mirdar proposed a radical idea: a single motion could be both an act of obedience and an act of disobedience depending on who performs it. He used examples to argue that God and humans could both be agents of the same act. This lesson presents his claim and al-Nasafi's dismissal of it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting al-Mirdar's two examples and al-Nasafi's closing dismissal.
Pay attention to the structure: claim, example 1, example 2, refutation.
2
Identify the core claim
Al-Mirdar asserts that a movement (ḥaraka) is the act of two agents and the object of power of both. Find the phrase 'فعلهما جميعًا ومقدورهما'.
This is the radical departure from orthodox view.
3
Examine the two examples
First example: commanded vs. forbidden agents. Second example: human forbidden vs. wind (God's creature). Understand how al-Mirdar attributes the same motion to both.
Note the moral labels: obedience/good for commanded, disobedience/evil for forbidden.
4
Understand al-Nasafi's refutation
Al-Nasafi ends by saying al-Mirdar has no argument against the People of Truth regarding the creation of acts. This implies his position is internally inconsistent.
The refutation is brief; the strength is in the orthodox alternative.
5
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this matters: if God's act can be disobedience from a human, it challenges God's justice. The orthodox view maintains a clear distinction between Creator and creature.
This connects to the broader debate on khalq al-afʿāl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو موسى بن صبح المردار المعروف بناسك البغداديين أن الحركة فعلهما جميعًا ومقدورهما ولو كان أحد المحركين مأمورا به والآخر منهيًا عنه كانت الحركة طاعة من المأمور به حسنا، معصية من المنهي عنه قبيحًا، ولو كان أحد المحركين إنسانًا منهيًا عن التحريك والآخر الريح كانت الحركة فعل الله تعالى وفعل العبد، وهي معصية قبيحة من العبد، وهي حكمة من الصانع القديم جل وعلا، وكان القديم فعل ما هو معصية من غيره ولم يكن بذلك عاصيًا، وكذا لو كان الرجل مأمورًا بذلك كان الله تعالى فعل ما هو طاعة من العبد، ولم يكن بذلك مطيعا وعلى هذا لم يبق له في مسألة خلق الأفعال مع أهل الحق كلام.
And Abu Musa ibn Subh al-Mirdar, known as the ascetic of the Baghdadis, claimed that motion (ḥaraka) is the act of both of them and the object of power of both of them. And if one of the movers was commanded to do it and the other forbidden from it, the motion would be obedience (ṭāʿa) from the one commanded—good, and disobedience (maʿṣiya) from the one forbidden—evil. And if one of the movers was a human forbidden from moving and the other the wind, then the motion would be the act of God Almighty and the act of the servant, and it would be an evil disobedience from the servant, and it would be wisdom from the Eternal Maker, and the Eternal did what is disobedience from other than Him and was not thereby disobedient. Likewise, if the man was commanded to do that, then God Almighty did what is obedience from the servant and was not thereby obedient. And on this, he has no argument against the People of Truth concerning the issue of the creation of acts (khalq al-afʿāl).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage reports the view of al-Mirdar, a Mu'tazilite theologian, who claimed that a single motion can be the act and object of power of two agents simultaneously. He provides examples to illustrate this: if one agent is commanded to move something and the other is forbidden, the motion is simultaneously obedience (from the commanded agent) and disobedience (from the forbidden agent). Even in a case where one mover is a human forbidden to move and the other is the wind (which is God's creature), the motion is attributed to both God and the human: it is an act of God and an act of the servant. The human's act is evil disobedience, while God's act is wise. Al-Nasafi then dismisses al-Mirdar's view by stating that he has no argument against Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox) regarding the creation of acts, implying that his position collapses into inconsistency.

Key Terms
حركة
فعلهما
مقدورهما
طاعة
معصية
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Mirdar, if a human is forbidden to move something and the wind moves it, the motion is:
A) Only God's act.
B) Only the human's act.
C) Both God's and the human's act.
D) Neither God's nor the human's act.

Key Takeaway
Al-Mirdar's view that a single act can be obedience and disobedience from different agents is rejected by al-Nasafi as inconsistent with orthodox understanding of divine creation of acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'God created this action in me,' and feel less responsible? How does the orthodox rejection of al-Mirdar's view remind you that your actions are truly yours, regardless of God's ultimate creation?

Abu al-Hudhayl's Claim on Motion Divisible by Agent
Al-Nasafi reports and refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that motion is divisible by agent but not in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu al-Hudhayl's attempt to avoid the contradiction of dual agency fails, preserving the refutation used against al-Mirdar.

Core Idea
Claiming motion is divisible by agent does not solve the problem; the act remains one.

Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, a leading Mu'tazilite, proposed a refinement to the earlier view of al-Mirdar. He argued that a single motion could be attributed to two agents in different respects without dividing the motion itself. Al-Nasafi, however, shows that this subtlety does not escape the same logical contradiction. Let's examine his claim and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu al-Hudhayl's claim
Read the passage carefully, noting how he distinguishes division in itself from division by agent.
He uses the example of a stone moved by two persons.
2
Identify al-Nasafi's refutation
Al-Nasafi points out that because the motion remains one in itself, the same contradiction arises: the act would be both obedience and disobedience.
3
Connect to the earlier refutation of al-Mirdar
Recognize that al-Nasafi says Abu al-Hudhayl is liable to all that al-Mirdar was liable to, meaning the problem persists.
This shows the consistency of the Maturidi critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو الهذيل العلاف أن حركة كل جزء من أجزاء الحجر مقسمة على كل واحد منهما لا في نفسها لأنها حركة واحدة وشيء واحد، والأعراض لا تنقسم في ذاتها، وإنما تنقسم بالمكان والزمان والفاعلين. ويلزمه جميع ما لزم المردار إذا كانت هي باعتبار ذاتها غير منقسمة فكانت هي باعتبار أحد الفاعلين طاعة وبالاعتبار الآخر معصية، وكذا إذا كان أحد المحركين ريحًا إذ هي في نفسها غير منقسمة فيلزمه جميع ما ألزمنا، ولم ينفعه هذا التجاهل.
And Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf claimed that the motion of each part of the stone is divided [between the two agents] in each of them, not in itself, because it is one motion and one thing, and accidents (aʿrāḍ) do not divide in their essence, but they divide by place, time, and agents. And he is liable to all that al-Mirdar was liable to, because if it is in itself indivisible, then it would be, in consideration of one agent, obedience (ṭāʿa), and in consideration of the other, disobedience (maʿṣiya). Likewise, if one of the movers is wind, since it is in itself indivisible, so he is liable to all that we have required, and this pretense of ignorance did not benefit him.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, another Mu'tazilite, who attempted to solve the problem raised against al-Mirdar. He claimed that the motion of each part of a stone is 'divided' between the two agents, but not in its essence—rather, it is divided by place, time, and agent. Since accidents (aʿrāḍ) are indivisible in themselves, they can only be distinguished by extrinsic factors. However, al-Nasafi refutes this by pointing out that because the motion is one and indivisible in itself, the same contradictions arise: from one agent's perspective it would be obedience, from another's disobedience, exactly as in al-Mirdar's case. The pretense of dividing by agent does not change the essential unity of the act, so Abu al-Hudhayl ends up with the same problems.

انقسام
فاعل
طاعة
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion can be divided by agent without affecting its unity.
Correction: Al-Nasafi demonstrates that essential unity remains, causing contradictory moral attributes.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi claim follows from Abu al-Hudhayl's view?
A) It leads to the same problems as al-Mirdar's view.
B) It provides a consistent solution.
C) It denies God's power.
D) It requires the division of accidents in essence.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Hudhayl's attempt to solve the dual agency problem by extrinsic division fails, confirming the Maturidi position that a single act cannot have two agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You face a moral choice where two influences conflict—your own desire and religious obligation. Is your act still one act? How do you resolve the tension between attributing it to yourself and to Allah's decree?
?
Abu al-Hudhayl tried to split a single motion by different agents. Where in your life are you trying to split your responsibility for an action between yourself and external factors? What would it mean to own it wholly?
?
The passage shows that dividing an act by agents doesn't remove the contradiction. Consider a sin you committed—do you ever tell yourself it was partly from shaytan or from your environment? How does this lesson call you to take full responsibility before Allah?

The Trilemma of Dividing Acts Between Two Agents
Al-Nasafi refutes Abu al-Hudhayl with a trilemma on divided acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's logical refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl's attempt to avoid the problem of two agents acting on a single act by dividing the act into portions.

Core Idea
Division of an act between agents leads to an inescapable trilemma.

Al-Nasafi now presses Abu al-Hudhayl with a precise logical dilemma. If an act like motion is divided between two agents, is the portion assigned to each the same or different? Whichever answer Abu al-Hudhayl gives, al-Nasafi shows it either collapses into his opponents' view or forces him into an unacceptable position. This passage is a masterclass in dialectical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Al-Nasafi asks: if motion is divided on the agents, is the portion of each agent the same as the other's portion or different?
This sets up two possible answers, later expanded to three.
2
Follow the first horn: same portion
If the portions are identical, then the act is effectively undivided, and the same contradictions (obedience/disobedience) persist. Read that part carefully.
3
Follow the second horn: different in expression, same in reality
If the portions are distinct only verbally but identical in reality, then Abu al-Hudhayl's position becomes equivalent to the Ash'ari distinction between kash and khalq, which he rejects.
This is a devastating tu quoque argument.
4
Follow the third horn: different in reality
If the portions are truly different in reality, that implies the divisibility of accidents, a view Abu al-Hudhayl does not hold but is held by Ja'far ibn Harb.
Thus all three options are blocked.
5
Reflect on the argument's structure
Notice how al-Nasafi forces his opponent into a trilemma with no escape. This is a classic example of qiyas al-taqsim (division by exhaustive enumeration).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نبين تجاهله فنقول له : إذا انقسمت الحركة على الفاعلين أكان قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه بعينه لا غير أم هو غير قسم صاحبه؟ فإن قال : هو قسم صاحبه، فإذا كان الفعل لهما لا على الانقسام، وكان على ما عليه قول خصومه من كونه طاعة ومعصية وفعلاً لفاعلين ومقدوراً لقادرين ولم يحصل الفرق بين انقسامها وامتناعها عن الانقسام، إذ لو قيل هي لا تنقسم لم يكن تحت هذا القول إلا هذا أن قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه. وإن قال: قسم كل واحد منهما غير قسم صاحبه في الذكر والعبارة إلا أن المنقسم واحد في الحقيقة. قيل : وخصومك يقولون : الكسب غير الخلق، وما عذب عليه غير ما لم يعذب عليه، وما قدر عليه الإنسان من الاكتساب غير ما لم يقدر عليه من الخلق في العبارة، والمخلوق هو المكتسب في الحقيقة، على هذا كان يتكلم حفص، فإن كان قولك هذا صحيحًا فقولهم كان صحيحا، وإن كان قولهم باطلاً كان قولك باطلاً. وإن أن أحد القسمين غير صاحبه على الحقيقة فهذا هو القول بتجزؤ الأعراض وانقسامها وهو لا يقول به، بل هو قول جعفر بن حرب ويقال له أيضا ما
Then we show his pretense of ignorance and say to him: If the motion is divided on the agents, is the portion (qism) of each of them the same as the portion of his companion exactly, or is it other than the portion of his companion? If he says: it is the portion of his companion, then if the act belongs to both of them without division, and it is what his opponents say concerning it being obedience and disobedience and an act of two agents and an object of power for two powers, then no difference occurs between its division and its prevention from division, because if it were said it does not divide, there would be nothing under this saying except that the portion of each of them is the portion of his companion. And if he says: the portion of each of them is other than the portion of his companion in mention and expression, but the divided is one in reality, then it is said: and your opponents say: acquisition (kash) is not creation (khalq), and what one is punished for is other than what one is not punished for, and what man has power over of acquisition is other than what he has no power over of creation in expression, and the created is the acquired in reality. On this basis did Hafs speak. So if your saying is correct, then their saying is correct, and if their saying is false, then your saying is false. And if [you say that] one of the two portions is other than its companion in reality, then this is the saying of the divisibility of accidents (tajazzuʾ al-aʿrāḍ) and their partition, and he does not hold this; rather, it is the saying of Ja'far ibn Harb. And it is also said to him...
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed logical refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl's position. The argument proceeds by asking whether the divided portion of the motion assigned to each agent is the same or different. If Abu al-Hudhayl says they are the same, then the act is effectively undivided, and the same contradictions (obedience and disobedience) arise as before. If he says they are different in expression but identical in reality, then his position becomes equivalent to the Ash'ari distinction between acquisition (kash) and creation (khalq), which he rejects. Thus his own argument would then validate his opponents' view. If he says they are truly different in reality, then that implies the divisibility of accidents (tajazzuʾ al-aʿrāḍ), which is not his own view but that of Ja'far ibn Harb. So Abu al-Hudhayl is forced into a trilemma, each horn either contradictory or leading to a position he does not hold. The passage ends with the beginning of a claim attributed to Ja'far ibn Harb, but the text is cut off.

انقسام
كسب
خلق
تجزؤ الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dividing an act into portions between two agents resolves the problem of two agents acting on a single act.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that division leads to three equally problematic outcomes, so division does not solve the problem.

Checkpoint
If Abu al-Hudhayl says the portions are different in expression but the same in reality, what consequence does al-Nasafi point out?
A) His view becomes identical to the Ash'ari view of kash and khalq.
B) He must accept the division of accidents.
C) He avoids all problems.
D) His view is still inconsistent with the example of the wind.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's trilemma demonstrates that the division of an act between two agents cannot escape the theological difficulties of multiple agents acting on a single event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever try to 'divide' the act between your own will and Allah's decree to lessen your feeling of responsibility? Read the trilemma again—how does it force you to accept full accountability for your actions? Will you own your sins completely today?
?
The trilemma shows that every option for dividing an act between agents leads to a problem. Are you willing to accept the full sovereignty of Allah over all actions, even when it challenges your logical comfort? What step will you take to submit more humbly to His decree?
?
If you believe that both you and Allah are 'agents' in your good deeds, do you ever feel a sense of pride in your role? How does this passage remind you that any 'division' is ultimately futile and that all true agency belongs to Allah alone? How will you realign your heart today?

The Indivisibility of Motion and Its Contradictions
Refutation of indivisible motion and dilemma about division among agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the claim that motion is indivisible in itself yet divided by agents, and the dilemma that exposes its inconsistency.

Core Idea
A single motion cannot be both indivisible and divided among agents without contradiction.

The author begins by stating the opponent's position: motion is a single accident, indivisible in itself, but divided among agents. He then shows that this leads to absurdities, such as a single act being both obedience and disobedience. He then poses a dilemma to force the opponent into either an impossible identity or an unwanted division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: statement, refutation, dilemma.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Spot the claim that motion is indivisible in itself yet divided by agents. Note how the author first illustrates the problem with al-Murdar's case and the wind example.
Al-Murdar was a Mu'tazilī with similar views.
3
Analyze the dilemma
The author asks: is the portion of each agent the same or different? Work through each option and its consequences. See how both options lead to problems for the opponent.
Option 1 collapses into the opponent's own position; Option 2 leads to a split in expression that the author will later refute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل واحد منهما لا في نفسها لأنها حركة واحدة وشيء واحد، والأعراض لا تنقسم في ذاتها، وإنما تنقسم بالمكان والزمان والفاعلين. ويلزمه جميع ما لزم المردار إذا كانت هي باعتبار ذاتها غير منقسمة فكانت هي باعتبار أحد الفاعلين طاعة وبالاعتبار الآخر معصية، وكذا إذا كان أحد المحركين ريحًا إذ هي في نفسها غير منقسمة فيلزمه جميع ما ألزمنا، ولم ينفعه هذا التجاهل.

ثم نبين تجاهله فنقول له : إذا انقسمت الحركة على الفاعلين أكان قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه بعينه لا غير أم هو غير قسم صاحبه؟

فإن قال : هو قسم صاحبه، فإذا كان الفعل لهما لا على الانقسام، وكان على ما عليه قول خصومه من كونه طاعة ومعصية وفعلاً لفاعلين ومقدوراً لقادرين ولم يحصل الفرق بين انقسامها وامتناعها عن الانقسام، إذ لو قيل هي لا تنقسم لم يكن تحت هذا القول إلا هذا أن قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه. وإن قال: قسم كل واحد منهما غير قسم صاحبه في الذكر والعبارة إلا أن المنقسم واحد في الحقيقة.

قيل : وخصومك يقولون : الكسب غير الخلق، وما عذب عليه غير ما لم يعذب عليه، وما قدر عليه الإنسان من الاكتساب غير ما لم يقدر عليه من الخلق في العبارة، والمخلوق هو المكتسب في الحقيقة، على هذا كان يتكلم حفص، فإن كان قولك هذا صحيحًا فقولهم كان صحيحا، وإن كان قولهم باطلاً كان قولك باطلاً. وإن أن أحد القسمين غير صاحبه على الحقيقة فهذا هو القول بتجزؤ الأعراض وانقسامها وهو لا يقول به، بل هو قول جعفر بن حرب ويقال له أيضا ما الفرق بينك وبين من يقول بتجزؤ الجزء فيجعل ما يلاقي الأرض غير ما يلاقي السماء، وما يلاقي الشمال غير ما يلاقي الجنوب، وما يلاقي الصبا غير ما يلاقي الدبور قياسًا على قسمتك الحركة الواحدة والشيء الواحد على مسببه لا في نفسه وهذا مما لا يجد فيه فرقا البتة.
Each one of them is not [divided] in itself because it is one motion and one thing, and accidents do not divide in their essence, but they divide by place, time, and agents. And there follows for him all that followed for al-Murdar: if they are, in consideration of their essence, indivisible, then they are, in consideration of one agent, obedience, and in the other consideration, disobedience. Likewise, if one of the movers is wind, since it is in itself indivisible, then there follows for him all that we have required, and this ignorance did not benefit him.

Then we expose his ignorance and say to him: When motion is divided among agents, is the portion of each one the very portion of his companion and nothing else, or is it other than the portion of his companion?

If he says: It is the portion of his companion, then the act is by both without division, and it would be according to the statement of his opponents—that it is obedience and disobedience, an act of two agents, and empowered by two empowerers—and no difference is obtained between its division and its prevention from division. For if it were said that it does not divide, this statement would only imply that the portion of each one is the portion of his companion. And if he says: The portion of each one is other than the portion of his companion in mention and expression, but the divided [whole] is one in reality.

It is said: And your opponents say: Acquisition (al-kasb) is other than creation (al-khalq), and what is punished for is other than what is not punished for, and what man is capable of in acquisition is other than what he is not capable of in creation in expression, but the created is the acquired in reality. It was in this way that Hafs used to speak. So if your statement is correct, then their statement is correct; and if their statement is false, then your statement is false. And if one of the two portions is other than its companion in reality, then this is the statement of the divisibility of accidents and their division, and he does not hold it. Rather, it is the statement of Ja'far ibn Harb. And it is also said to him: What difference is there between you and one who says that the atom divides, making what faces the earth other than what faces the sky, and what faces the north other than what faces the south, and what faces the east wind other than what faces the west wind, by analogy to your division of the single motion and single thing according to its causes, not in itself? And this is something for which he finds no difference at all.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific opponent's claim regarding the division of motion. The author begins by stating the opponent's position: that each motion (like obedience and disobedience) is not divided in itself because it is a single motion, and accidents (a'rād) do not divide in their essence, but they divide by place, time, and agents. The author then points out that this leads the opponent into the same difficulties faced by al-Murdar (a Mu'tazilī) after him: if the motion is indivisible in itself, then it becomes obedience by one agent and disobedience by another, which is contradictory. Similarly, if one mover is wind, the same problem arises. This ignorance does not help him.

Next, the author exposes the ignorance further by posing a dilemma: when motion is divided among agents, is each agent's portion exactly the same as the other's, or is it different? If the opponent says it is the same, then the act belongs to both without division, and the opponent's position becomes identical to that of his opponents (who hold that a single act can be both obedience and disobedience, an act of two agents, and empowered by two empowerers). Thus, no difference remains between saying the motion divides and saying it does not divide. If the opponent says the portions are different in expression but the divided whole is one in reality, the author counters by drawing an analogy to the kasb (acquisition) doctrine of the opponents (likely the Ash'aris or early Māturīdīs): they say that kasb is other than khalq in expression, but the created is the acquired in reality. This is how Hafs (al-Fard) spoke. So if the opponent's distinction is valid, then the kasb doctrine is also valid; if the kasb doctrine is false, then the opponent's distinction is false.

Finally, if the opponent insists that the two portions are truly distinct, he is forced into the position of accident divisibility (tajazzu' al-a'rād), which he does not accept. That position is held by Ja'far ibn Ḥarb. The author then presses further: how is his division different from the division of an atom? Just as someone might divide an atom into parts facing different directions, so too does the opponent divide a single motion by its causes, not in itself. The opponent can find no real difference. Thus, the author has trapped the opponent in an inconsistent position.

Key Terms
حركة
انقسام
طاعة
معصية
فاعل
مردار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is arguing for the division of accidents.
Correction: The author is refuting an opponent who inconsistently claims motion is indivisible in itself yet divided by agents.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does the author pose to the opponent regarding the division of motion among agents?
A) Whether motion can be created by two agents simultaneously
B) Whether each agent's portion is identical or different
C) Whether motion is an accident or a substance
D) Whether time and place affect the division of motion

Key Takeaway
Motion cannot be both indivisible in essence and divided among agents; the dilemma shows that neither option saves the opponent's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you considered a single action of yours as both pleasing and displeasing to Allah. How does this lesson's argument challenge your assumption that one action can have two contradictory moral evaluations? Are you willing to accept that your action must be either obedience or disobedience, and if so, what does that mean for the way you reflect on your choices?
?
The opponent claimed that motion is indivisible yet divided by agents. In your own life, do you ever attribute your actions to both yourself and Allah without truly understanding how that works? How does this dilemma push you to clarify your belief about the relationship between your will and Allah's will in every movement you make?
?
If every motion of your body is ultimately created by Allah, but you are still accountable for it, how do you reconcile the tension between divine agency and personal responsibility? The Kasb Analogy and the Reductio to Accident Divisibility
Refutation via kasb analogy and reductio to accident divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's use of the kasb analogy to refute the opponent's distinction between 'same in reality' and 'different in expression,' and the reductio ad absurdum to accident divisibility.

Core Idea
If the opponent accepts different portions in expression, he cannot distinguish himself from the kasb doctrine he rejects.

The author continues his refutation by addressing the opponent's second response: that the portions are different in expression but the same in reality. He draws an analogy to the kasb (acquisition) doctrine of his opponents, showing that if the opponent's reasoning is sound, then kasb is also sound. He then shows that if the portions are truly different, the opponent falls into accident divisibility, which he denies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Trace the argument: opponent's response, kasb analogy, reductio, atom analogy.
2
Understand the kasb analogy
Note how Hafs al-Fard distinguished kasb and khalq: different in expression but same in reality. The author uses this to mirror the opponent's distinction.
This is a common argumentative technique: parallel reasoning.
3
Examine the reductio
If the opponent insists the portions are truly different, he must accept accident divisibility (tajazzu' al-a'rād), which he rejects. The author then likens this to dividing an atom into parts facing different directions.
Ja'far ibn Harb is known for holding accident divisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل واحد منهما لا في نفسها لأنها حركة واحدة وشيء واحد، والأعراض لا تنقسم في ذاتها، وإنما تنقسم بالمكان والزمان والفاعلين. ويلزمه جميع ما لزم المردار إذا كانت هي باعتبار ذاتها غير منقسمة فكانت هي باعتبار أحد الفاعلين طاعة وبالاعتبار الآخر معصية، وكذا إذا كان أحد المحركين ريحًا إذ هي في نفسها غير منقسمة فيلزمه جميع ما ألزمنا، ولم ينفعه هذا التجاهل.

ثم نبين تجاهله فنقول له : إذا انقسمت الحركة على الفاعلين أكان قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه بعينه لا غير أم هو غير قسم صاحبه؟

فإن قال : هو قسم صاحبه، فإذا كان الفعل لهما لا على الانقسام، وكان على ما عليه قول خصومه من كونه طاعة ومعصية وفعلاً لفاعلين ومقدوراً لقادرين ولم يحصل الفرق بين انقسامها وامتناعها عن الانقسام، إذ لو قيل هي لا تنقسم لم يكن تحت هذا القول إلا هذا أن قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه. وإن قال: قسم كل واحد منهما غير قسم صاحبه في الذكر والعبارة إلا أن المنقسم واحد في الحقيقة.

قيل : وخصومك يقولون : الكسب غير الخلق، وما عذب عليه غير ما لم يعذب عليه، وما قدر عليه الإنسان من الاكتساب غير ما لم يقدر عليه من الخلق في العبارة، والمخلوق هو المكتسب في الحقيقة، على هذا كان يتكلم حفص، فإن كان قولك هذا صحيحًا فقولهم كان صحيحا، وإن كان قولهم باطلاً كان قولك باطلاً. وإن أن أحد القسمين غير صاحبه على الحقيقة فهذا هو القول بتجزؤ الأعراض وانقسامها وهو لا يقول به، بل هو قول جعفر بن حرب ويقال له أيضا ما الفرق بينك وبين من يقول بتجزؤ الجزء فيجعل ما يلاقي الأرض غير ما يلاقي السماء، وما يلاقي الشمال غير ما يلاقي الجنوب، وما يلاقي الصبا غير ما يلاقي الدبور قياسًا على قسمتك الحركة الواحدة والشيء الواحد على مسببه لا في نفسه وهذا مما لا يجد فيه فرقا البتة.
Each one of them is not [divided] in itself because it is one motion and one thing, and accidents do not divide in their essence, but they divide by place, time, and agents. And there follows for him all that followed for al-Murdar: if they are, in consideration of their essence, indivisible, then they are, in consideration of one agent, obedience, and in the other consideration, disobedience. Likewise, if one of the movers is wind, since it is in itself indivisible, then there follows for him all that we have required, and this ignorance did not benefit him.

Then we expose his ignorance and say to him: When motion is divided among agents, is the portion of each one the very portion of his companion and nothing else, or is it other than the portion of his companion?

If he says: It is the portion of his companion, then the act is by both without division, and it would be according to the statement of his opponents—that it is obedience and disobedience, an act of two agents, and empowered by two empowerers—and no difference is obtained between its division and its prevention from division. For if it were said that it does not divide, this statement would only imply that the portion of each one is the portion of his companion. And if he says: The portion of each one is other than the portion of his companion in mention and expression, but the divided [whole] is one in reality.

It is said: And your opponents say: Acquisition (al-kasb) is other than creation (al-khalq), and what is punished for is other than what is not punished for, and what man is capable of in acquisition is other than what he is not capable of in creation in expression, but the created is the acquired in reality. It was in this way that Hafs used to speak. So if your statement is correct, then their statement is correct; and if their statement is false, then your statement is false. And if one of the two portions is other than its companion in reality, then this is the statement of the divisibility of accidents and their division, and he does not hold it. Rather, it is the statement of Ja'far ibn Harb. And it is also said to him: What difference is there between you and one who says that the atom divides, making what faces the earth other than what faces the sky, and what faces the north other than what faces the south, and what faces the east wind other than what faces the west wind, by analogy to your division of the single motion and single thing according to its causes, not in itself? And this is something for which he finds no difference at all.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific opponent's claim regarding the division of motion. The author begins by stating the opponent's position: that each motion (like obedience and disobedience) is not divided in itself because it is a single motion, and accidents (a'rād) do not divide in their essence, but they divide by place, time, and agents. The author then points out that this leads the opponent into the same difficulties faced by al-Murdar (a Mu'tazilī) after him: if the motion is indivisible in itself, then it becomes obedience by one agent and disobedience by another, which is contradictory. Similarly, if one mover is wind, the same problem arises. This ignorance does not help him.

Next, the author exposes the ignorance further by posing a dilemma: when motion is divided among agents, is each agent's portion exactly the same as the other's, or is it different? If the opponent says it is the same, then the act belongs to both without division, and the opponent's position becomes identical to that of his opponents (who hold that a single act can be both obedience and disobedience, an act of two agents, and empowered by two empowerers). Thus, no difference remains between saying the motion divides and saying it does not divide. If the opponent says the portions are different in expression but the divided whole is one in reality, the author counters by drawing an analogy to the kasb (acquisition) doctrine of the opponents (likely the Ash'aris or early Māturīdīs): they say that kasb is other than khalq in expression, but the created is the acquired in reality. This is how Hafs (al-Fard) spoke. So if the opponent's distinction is valid, then the kasb doctrine is also valid; if the kasb doctrine is false, then the opponent's distinction is false.

Finally, if the opponent insists that the two portions are truly distinct, he is forced into the position of accident divisibility (tajazzu' al-a'rād), which he does not accept. That position is held by Ja'far ibn Ḥarb. The author then presses further: how is his division different from the division of an atom? Just as someone might divide an atom into parts facing different directions, so too does the opponent divide a single motion by its causes, not in itself. The opponent can find no real difference. Thus, the author has trapped the opponent in an inconsistent position.

Key Terms
كسب
خلق
حفص
جعفر بن حرب
تجزؤ
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author endorses the kasb doctrine.
Correction: The author uses the kasb doctrine as a logical parallel to show that the opponent's reasoning leads to an undesired conclusion.

Checkpoint
What does the author use the kasb analogy to show?
A) That the opponent's distinction is invalid
B) That kasb is the correct doctrine
C) That motion is divisible in essence
D) That Ja'far ibn Harb is correct

Key Takeaway
The opponent cannot maintain a distinction between 'same in reality' and 'different in expression' without either admitting the kasb doctrine or falling into accident divisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author exposes a flaw in the opponent's reasoning that seemed plausible at first. Think of an assumption you hold about your own faith or practice that you have never rigorously examined. Are you willing to scrutinize it with the same logical honesty? What one step will you take this week to test that assumption?
?
The argument hinges on the principle that if two things are truly the same in reality, they cannot be treated differently. Where in your life do you make arbitrary distinctions—between 'sacred' and 'mundane' tasks, or between how you treat different people—that may not align with their true reality before Allah? How can you begin to align your actions with the truth?
?
The opponent's distinction collapsed under pressure, forcing a choice between two unwanted consequences. When you face a difficult choice in your spiritual life, do you look for convenient middle-ground answers rather than accepting the challenging truth? Commit to asking Allah for guidance to accept the truth even when it makes you uncomfortable.

Refutation of the Division of the Act between God and Servant
Al-Nasafi refutes the three-part division of the servant's act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God must be blamed for evil acts if He creates them is invalid, and how the Maturidi response distinguishes between creation and acquisition.

Core Idea
Division of acts into parts applies to bodies, not to actions.

Imam al-Nasafi addresses a common objection from the Mu'tazila: if God creates all acts, then He must be responsible for evil. The opponents propose a three-part division, but al-Nasafi shows this division is logically flawed because actions are accidents and cannot be partitioned. He then strengthens his argument with a striking example from Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Murdar.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's objection
Read the opening lines where the opponents present their three-part division: the act is either entirely from God, entirely from the servant, or partly from both.
This division seems plausible but is built on a mistaken assumption.
2
Grasp the first refutation
Notice that al-Nasafi rejects the division itself. He explains that such division applies only to bodies that are divisible, but actions are accidents and cannot be divided.
Accidents have no parts; thus the whole-part distinction is invalid.
3
Follow the second argument
Al-Nasafi then questions the opponents on their own terms: even God's effect (the act) is a 'thing' and 'existent,' but they only blame God for existence, not for thingness. Similarly, we distinguish God's creation from the servant's acquisition.
This shows consistency in the Maturidi position.
4
See the consequentialist argument
Finally, al-Nasafi shows that the opponents' own principles lead to absurdity: a single movement can be both obedience and disobedience depending on the agent, proving that the act is one in itself but takes different rulings.
This refutes the necessity of dividing the act into parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن فعل العبد لا يخلو إما أن يكون كله من الله تعالى فيذم هو عليه، وإما أن يكون كله من العبد وهو ما قلنا، وإما أن يكون بعضه من الله تعالى وبعضه من العبد فيشتركان في الذم. فيقال لهم: لو عقلتم لما اشتغلتم بهذا التقسيم إذ هذا مما يصح في الأجسام لما تتبعض وتتجزأ دون الأعراض التي يستحيل عليها التبعض والتجزؤ لانعدام تألفها وتركبها، وما لا بعض له لا كل له إذ هما من الأسماء الإضافية والتقسيم إلى الكل والبعض، فما يستحيل عليه البعض والكل جهل. ثم نقول لهم: هل مفعول الله تعالى وهو فعله فيذم على فعله كما هو عندكم شيء وذات وعين وموجود ويذم على وجوده دون شيئيته وذاتيته وعينيته، على أنا إذا أثبتنا بالدليل أن له فعلاً لا اختراع له فيذم هو على فعله ولا يذم الله تعالى على اختراعه لما نبين بعد هذا إن شاء الله تعالى كما يذم عندكم على وجود فعله دون شيئيته على أن هذا يلزم أبا الهذيل وأبا موسى المردار حيث جعلا حركة الحجر فعلاً للمحركين، ولو كان أحدهما مأمورًا بالتحريك والآخر منهيًا عنه فتكون الحركة في ذاتها شيئًا واحدًا وهي طاعة ومعصية يحمد المطيع منهما عليها ويثاب ويذم العاصي منهما عليها ويعاقب، وكذا لو كان المحركين الريح كانت الحركة في نفسها فعلاً لله تعالى ويحمد عليه وفعلاً للمحرك المنهي عنه ومعصية منه يذم عليها ويعاقب، وإن لم تكن هي متجزئة في نفسها، والله الموفق.
And with this they are answered concerning their statement: 'The act of the servant is not free from being either entirely from God—so He is blamed for it—or entirely from the servant—which is what we said—or partly from God and partly from the servant—so they share in blame.' So it is said to them: 'If you understood, you would not occupy yourselves with this division, for this is valid for bodies because they are divisible and partitioned, unlike accidents, for which partition and division are impossible due to the absence of composition and structure in them. And that which has no part has no whole, since they are relational terms, and division into whole and part. So that for which part and whole are impossible is ignorance.' Then we say to them: 'Is the effect of God—which is His act—something, a substance, an entity, and an existent, as it is with you, and is He blamed for its existence without [blame for] its thingness, substantiality, and entity? Given that we have proven by evidence that He has an act that is not His creation, so He is blamed for His act, and God is not blamed for His creation, as we will explain after this—God willing—just as with you He is blamed for the existence of His act without its thingness. Moreover, this necessarily follows for Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Musa al-Murdar, for they made the movement of a stone an act of the movers. If one of them was commanded to move and the other forbidden, then the movement would be one thing in itself, and it would be obedience and disobedience; the obedient one would be praised and rewarded, and the disobedient one would be blamed and punished. Similarly, if the movers were the wind, the movement would be in itself an act of God—praised for it—and an act of the forbidden mover, and disobedience from him for which he would be blamed and punished, even though it is not divided in itself. And God is the Grantor of success.'
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents argue that the servant's act must be either entirely from God (thus God is blamed), entirely from the servant (which we affirm), or partly from each (so both share blame). The answer first refutes the validity of this division: such division applies to bodies that are divisible, but accidents (like actions) cannot be divided because they lack composition. Since they have no parts, the whole-part distinction does not apply. Then a second argument: Even on the opponents' own view, God's effect (the act) is a thing, substance, etc., but they only blame Him for its existence, not for its quiddity. Similarly, we hold that God creates the act but does not perform it as His own act; blame goes to the servant for the act, not to God for creation. The author then shows that this reasoning leads to absurd consequences for Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Murdar: they consider the movement of a stone as the act of the mover, even when the same movement is both obedience and disobedience depending on the agent. This proves that the act is one in itself but takes different rulings based on the agent, refuting their division.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
تقسيم
أعراض
تبعض
اختراع
مفعول
ذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all acts, He must be the agent of evil and thus blameworthy.
Correction: God creates the act as an accident, but the servant acquires it (kasb). The servant is blamed because he chooses and intends the act; God is not blameworthy for creation itself.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Nasafi reject the opponents' division of the act into being entirely from God, entirely from the servant, or partly from each?
A) Because the Qur'an forbids such division.
B) Because accidents cannot be divided into parts, so the division is invalid.
C) Because the servant has no real power over his act.
D) Because God's act is not comparable to the servant's act.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's division is invalid because actions are indivisible accidents; blame is on the servant for acquisition, not on God for creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever internally shift blame to Allah, thinking 'He made me do it' or 'He created my circumstances'? How does al-Nasafi's separation of creation from acquisition challenge you to fully own your actions starting today?
?
If actions cannot be divided — not partly God, partly you — then every sin is entirely your responsibility. Does this truth unsettle you? How will you change your repentance so that it includes a firm resolve not to excuse yourself?
?
Al-Nasafi says even the opponents only blame God for the 'existence' of the act, not its essence. Have you ever criticized Allah's decree in your heart, attributing something 'bad' to Him while forgetting that He is not blameworthy? What concrete step will you take to guard your tongue and heart against this?
